template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Hydraulic fracturing in the United Kingdom</b> started in the late 1970s with fracturing of the conventional oil and gas fields of the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>.  It has been used in about 200&#160;British onshore oil and gas wells since the early 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-RAEreport_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RAEreport-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> The technique did not attract attention until licences use were awarded for onshore <a href="/wiki/Shale_gas" title="Shale gas">shale gas</a> exploration in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-BGS_2010_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BGS_2010-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBC_20Jan2011_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_20Jan2011-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the United Kingdom, as in other countries\xe2\x80\x94and <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_States" title="Hydraulic fracturing in the United States">in particular the United States</a>, where the industry is most advanced and widespread, hydraulic fracturing has generated a large amount of controversy.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Although <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_fracturing" title="Hydraulic fracturing">hydraulic fracturing</a> is often used synonymously to refer to shale gas and other <a href="/wiki/Unconventional_oil" title="Unconventional oil">unconventional oil</a> and gas sources, it is not always correct to associate it with <a href="/wiki/Unconventional_gas" title="Unconventional gas">unconventional gas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-SB1368_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SB1368-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In late May 2011, the first UK exploration for shale gas using high-volume hydraulic fracturing was suspended at Preese Hall in Lancashire after the process triggered two minor earthquakes.<sup id="cite_ref-DECCFeb2014_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DECCFeb2014-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The larger of the earthquakes caused minor deformation of the wellbore<sup id="cite_ref-well_deform_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-well_deform-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> and was strong enough to be felt.<sup id="cite_ref-White_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-White-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The report of 2012 by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Academy_of_Engineering" title="Royal Academy of Engineering">Royal Academy of Engineering</a> concluded that earthquake risk was minimal, and recommended the process be given nationwide clearance, although it highlighted certain concerns<sup id="cite_ref-RAEreport_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RAEreport-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> which lead to changes in regulations.<sup id="cite_ref-DECCreg_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DECCreg-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In January 2014, the European Commission issued a set of recommendations on the minimum principles for the exploration and production of hydrocarbons from shale formations using high-volume hydraulic fracturing.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Updated guidance for the public<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="Needs summarising to remain in the lead (March 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> was issued in January 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-BEISGuidance2017_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BEISGuidance2017-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" href="#Environmental_impact"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Environmental impact</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-27"><a href="#Air"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">Air</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-28"><a href="#Water"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">Water</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-29"><a href="#Groundwater_contamination"><span class="tocnumber">6.2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Groundwater contamination</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-30"><a href="#UK_and_US_water_differences"><span class="tocnumber">6.2.2</span> <span class="toctext">UK and US water differences</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-31"><a href="#Water_use"><span class="tocnumber">6.2.3</span> <span class="toctext">Water use</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-32"><a href="#Seismicity"><span class="tocnumber">6.3</span> <span class="toctext">Seismicity</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-33"><a href="#Preese_Hall,_Lancashire"><span class="tocnumber">6.3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Preese Hall, Lancashire</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-34"><a href="#Subsidence"><span class="tocnumber">6.3.2</span> <span class="toctext">Subsidence</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-35"><a href="#Insurance"><span class="tocnumber">6.3.3</span> <span class="toctext">Insurance</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-36"><a href="#Public_health"><span class="tocnumber">6.4</span> <span class="toctext">Public health</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-37"><a href="#The_&#39;Fracking&#39;_debate"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">The \'Fracking\' debate</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-38"><a href="#Opposition_and_support_for_fracking"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Opposition and support for fracking</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-39"><a href="#Advertising_Standards_Authority_complaints"><span class="tocnumber">7.2</span> <span class="toctext">Advertising Standards Authority complaints</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-40"><a href="#Wales"><span class="tocnumber">7.3</span> <span class="toctext">Wales</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-41"><a href="#Industry_response"><span class="tocnumber">7.4</span> <span class="toctext">Industry response</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-42"><a href="#Effect_on_house_prices"><span class="tocnumber">7.5</span> <span class="toctext">Effect on house prices</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-43"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-44"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-45"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-46"><a href="#Videos"><span class="tocnumber">10.1</span> <span class="toctext">Videos</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oil_well_in_Lincolnshire_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1139065.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Oil_well_in_Lincolnshire_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1139065.jpg/220px-Oil_well_in_Lincolnshire_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1139065.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Oil_well_in_Lincolnshire_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1139065.jpg/330px-Oil_well_in_Lincolnshire_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1139065.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Oil_well_in_Lincolnshire_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1139065.jpg/440px-Oil_well_in_Lincolnshire_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1139065.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oil_well_in_Lincolnshire_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1139065.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Oil well in Lincolnshire. Around 200&#160;onshore wells such as this have been hydraulically fractured.</div></div></div>\n<p>The first experimental use of hydraulic fracturing in the world was in 1947, and the first commercially successful applications of hydraulic fracturing were in 1949 in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-Montgomery_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Montgomery-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Offshore">Offshore</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Offshore">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In the United Kingdom, the first hydraulic fracturing of an oil well was carried out shortly after discovery of the <a href="/wiki/West_Sole" class="mw-redirect" title="West Sole">West Sole</a> field in the North Sea in 1965.  After the industry started to use intermediate- and high-strength proppants in late 1970s, hydraulic fracturing became a common technique in the North Sea oil and gas wells. The first hydraulic fracturing from ship was conducted in the British Southern North Sea in 1980, with <a href="/wiki/High-volume_hydraulic_fracturing" class="mw-redirect" title="High-volume hydraulic fracturing">massive or high volume hydraulic fracturing</a> used from 1984 onwards.<sup id="cite_ref-Mader_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mader-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Onshore">Onshore</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Onshore">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>An estimated 200&#160;conventional onshore wells have been subject to low volume hydraulic fracturing; around 10% of all onshore wells in the United Kingdom,<sup id="cite_ref-RAEreport_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RAEreport-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> including <a href="/wiki/Wytch_Farm" title="Wytch Farm">Wytch Farm</a>, which is the largest onshore conventional oil field in western Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The surge of public interest in high-volume hydraulic fracturing in the UK can be traced to 2008, when <a href="/wiki/Cuadrilla_Resources" title="Cuadrilla Resources">Cuadrilla Resources</a> was granted a petroleum exploration and development licence in the 13th onshore licensing round for unconventional <a href="/wiki/Shale_gas_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Shale gas in the United Kingdom">shale gas exploration</a> along the coast of <a href="/wiki/Lancashire" title="Lancashire">Lancashire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-iee310714_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iee310714-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBC_20Jan2011_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_20Jan2011-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The company\'s first and only high-volume hydraulic fracturing job<sup id="cite_ref-DECCwater_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DECCwater-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>4</span></sup> was performed in March 2011, near Blackpool, Lancashire.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_28Mar2011_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_28Mar2011-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hendry_22Sep2011_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hendry_22Sep2011-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Cuadrilla halted operations in May 2011 at their Lancashire drilling site due to seismic activity damaging the casing in the production zone.<sup id="cite_ref-Macalister_13Mar2013_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Macalister_13Mar2013-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>From 1977 until 1994, a <a href="/wiki/Hot_dry_rock_geothermal_energy" title="Hot dry rock geothermal energy">hot dry rock geothermal energy</a> experiment was conducted in the <a href="/wiki/Carnmenellis" title="Carnmenellis">Carnmenellis</a> granite of Cornwall. During that experiment, three geothermal wells with depth of 2.6 kilometres (1.6&#160;mi) were hydraulically fractured "to research the hydraulic stimulation of fracture networks at temperatures below 100&#160;\xc2\xb0C (212&#160;\xc2\xb0F)".<sup id="cite_ref-Busby2010_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Busby2010-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Process">Process</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Process">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Hydraulic fracturing is a <a href="/wiki/Well-stimulation" class="mw-redirect" title="Well-stimulation">well-stimulation</a> technique in which <a href="/wiki/Source_rock" title="Source rock">rock</a> is fractured by a <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydraulic">hydraulically</a> pressurized fluid. This process is also known as \'fracking\'. Hydraulic fracturing requires a <a href="/wiki/Borehole" title="Borehole">borehole</a> to be drilled to target depth in the <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_reservoir" title="Petroleum reservoir">reservoir</a>. For oil and gas production, hydraulically fractured wells can be <a href="/wiki/Horizontal_drilling" class="mw-redirect" title="Horizontal drilling">horizontal</a> or vertical, while the reservoir can be conventional or unconventional. After the well has been <a href="/wiki/Well_drilling" title="Well drilling">drilled</a>, <a href="/wiki/Completion_(oil_and_gas_wells)#Cased_hole_completion" title="Completion (oil and gas wells)">lined</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Well_logging" title="Well logging">geophysically logged</a>, the rock can be hydraulically fractured.<sup id="cite_ref-NERC2015_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NERC2015-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Method">Method</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Method">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_fracturing#Method" title="Hydraulic fracturing">Hydraulic fracturing \xc2\xa7&#160;Method</a></div>\n<p>There are six stages in hydraulic fracturing: Perforation; Isolation: Stimulation; Flushing; Multi-stage perforation, and; Flowback.<sup id="cite_ref-NERC2015_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NERC2015-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Perforation">Perforation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Perforation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In shale plays, the cased well is perforated using "shaped charges (explosives)", which are detonated at selected locations in the production zone. In addition to making perforations in the casing, these detonations also create "finger-like fractures" "up to 2.5 cm in diameter" that "extend up to 60 cm into the formation".<sup id="cite_ref-NERC2015_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NERC2015-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the United Kingdom, geothermal wells normally use <a href="/wiki/Completion_(oil_and_gas_wells)#Barefoot_completion" title="Completion (oil and gas wells)">barefoot completions</a>, rather than "perforated and cemented completions" within a cased production zone.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Isolation">Isolation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Isolation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Each perforated section is isolated using a <a href="/wiki/Production_packer" title="Production packer">packer</a> (seal)<sup id="cite_ref-NERC2015_21-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NERC2015-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Stimulation">Stimulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Stimulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>For both low and high volume hydraulic fracturing stimulation of a hydrocarbon well, a high-pressure fluid (usually water) containing chemical additives and a <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_fracturing_proppants" title="Hydraulic fracturing proppants">proppant</a> is injected into a <a href="/wiki/Wellbore" class="mw-redirect" title="Wellbore">wellbore</a> to create an extensive system of <a href="/wiki/Fracture_(geology)#Tensile_fractures" title="Fracture (geology)">small cracks</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Geological_formation" title="Geological formation">deep-rock formations</a>. These cracks provide the pathway for: <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>, (including <a href="/wiki/Shale_gas" title="Shale gas">shale gas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tight_gas" title="Tight gas">tight gas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coalbed_methane" title="Coalbed methane">coalbed methane</a>); <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a>, (including <a href="/wiki/Light_tight_oil" class="mw-redirect" title="Light tight oil">shale or tight oil</a>); to flow more freely. When the hydraulic pressure is removed from the well, the small grains of hydraulic fracturing proppant<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> hold the fractures open when the pressure is released.<sup id="cite_ref-NERC2015_21-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NERC2015-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>When a hydrocarbon well is hydraulically fractured, this is done through a <a href="/wiki/Production_packer" title="Production packer">production packer</a> (seal), through the drill pipe or tubing. Fluids are circulated down the tubing, to below the point where the packer is sealed against the production casing. Pressure is then be applied only that part of the casing below the packer.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The rest of the well casing will not experience any increase in pressure due to the sealing of the packer. The surface casings do not experience the great pressures experienced at the production zone. This means the stresses on a surface casing are no greater than on a normal oil or gas well. Smaller diameter pipes can sustain much larger pressures than large diameter pipes.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>In HDR geothermal hydraulic fracturing stimulation, proppants are not added to hydraulic fracturing fluid, as the rough-surfaced <a href="/wiki/Fracture_(geology)#Faults_and_shear_fractures" title="Fracture (geology)">shear fractures</a> stay open through self-propping.<sup id="cite_ref-Busby2010_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Busby2010-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><br />\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Flushing">Flushing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Flushing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Multi-stage_perforation">Multi-stage perforation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Multi-stage perforation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In horizontally drilled sections, it is common to perform as many as 30 separate fracture stages, to evenly divide the <a href="/wiki/Completion_(oil_and_gas_wells)#Lower_completion" title="Completion (oil and gas wells)">production zone</a>. In multi-stage fracturing, segments of a horizontal well, starting at the end furthest from the well head, are split into isolated segments and fractured separately.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Flowback">Flowback</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Flowback">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Flowback_fluid">Flowback fluid</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Flowback fluid">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<p>Flowback fluid contains high levels of salt and is contaminated with organic "solids, heavy metals, fracking chemicals and <a href="/wiki/Naturally_occurring_radioactive_material" title="Naturally occurring radioactive material">naturally occurring radioactive material</a> (NORM) of varying concentration and low levels of <a href="/wiki/Radioactive" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioactive">radioactive</a> materials".<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The Environment Agency strategy for management of NORM-contaminated flowback fluid, after treatment, includes its preferred re-use by re-injection during hydraulic fracturing<sup id="cite_ref-OOGSG1_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OOGSG1-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> and its disposal, with caveats, via water treatment sites.<sup id="cite_ref-NORM_TreatDisp_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NORM_TreatDisp-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Flowback fluid can be treated and reused in later hydraulic fracturing operations,<sup id="cite_ref-OOGSG1_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OOGSG1-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> to reduce the volume of freshwater required and to mitigate issues arising from off-site disposal of flowback fluid.<sup id="cite_ref-GreenStylesBaptie_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GreenStylesBaptie-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\nFlowback fluid injection in deep disposal wells, which has been linked to significant increase in earthquake rate,<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> is not currently permitted in the UK by the Environment Agency.<sup id="cite_ref-OOGSG1_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OOGSG1-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Research by Engelder et alia in 2012, indicated that any water injected into a shale formation that does not flow back to the surface, known as "residual treatment water", would be permanently absorbed, (sequestered) into the shale.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In January 2014, "applications for permits to frack" were withdrawn by Cuadrilla after arrangements for treatment and disposal of NORM-contaminated flowback fluid were considered inadequate by the Environment Agency.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Technologies are developing methods of removing salt and radioactive materials, allowing safe disposal of flowback fluid under Environment Agency licence.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Research in the US also indicates new methods such as "microbial capacitive desalination cells" may become available.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fracture_fluids">Fracture fluids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Fracture fluids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_fracturing#Fracturing_fluids" title="Hydraulic fracturing">Hydraulic_fracturing \xc2\xa7&#160;Fracturing fluids</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/List_of_additives_for_hydraulic_fracturing#United_Kingdom" title="List of additives for hydraulic fracturing">Chemical additives</a>, typically around 1 per cent of the total fluid volume, are added to water to reduce water viscosity and modify fluid properties.<sup id="cite_ref-Petrowiki_frack_fluids_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petrowiki_frack_fluids-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> The fracturing fluid used at Preese Hall 1 was "99.95% water and sand".<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> The chemical additives (0.05 per cent) were:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Polyacrylamide" title="Polyacrylamide">Polyacrylamide</a> emulsion in hydrocarbon oil (0.043 per cent), which reduces the viscosity of the water to allow faster pumping. It is classed as a "non-hazardous pollutant"<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>Sodium salt, for tracing fracturing fluid (0.000005 per cent)<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>Proppants may comprise up to 10 per cent of hydraulic fracturing fluid volume.<sup id="cite_ref-Petrowiki_frack_fluids_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petrowiki_frack_fluids-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> The proppants used at Preese Hall 1 were silica sand: \n</p>\n<ul><li>Congleton Sand (0.473 per cent)</li>\n<li>Chelford Sand (1.550 per cent)<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>Additional chemical additives that were permitted at Preese Hall 1, but not used, were highly dilute <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glutaraldehyde" title="Glutaraldehyde">glutaraldehyde</a>, which is used as a biocide in very small quantities, to sterilise the water.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_germicidal_irradiation" title="Ultraviolet germicidal irradiation">Ultraviolet germicidal irradiation</a> is another replacement available for water sterilisation.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\nAlthough some of the chemicals used in hydraulic fracturing fluids such as hydrochloric acid may be classified as toxic, corrosive or irritant,<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> they are non-toxic at lower concentrations.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Waterless_fracturing_fluid_systems">Waterless fracturing fluid systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Waterless fracturing fluid systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Other fracturing fluid systems<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> such as gels, foams and compressed gases, including <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and air, can be injected in place of water. Waterless fracturing fluids that use propane-based <a href="/wiki/Liquefied_petroleum_gas" title="Liquefied petroleum gas">LPG</a><sup id="cite_ref-GasFrac_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GasFrac-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> have the potential to reduce wastewater toxicity.<sup id="cite_ref-RAEreport_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RAEreport-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>20</span></sup> There is sometimes a need to hydraulically fracture <a href="/wiki/Coalbed_methane" title="Coalbed methane">coalbed methane</a> and these<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="specify which methods (April 2017)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> methods can be used.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fracture_monitoring">Fracture monitoring</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Fracture monitoring">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_fracturing#Fracture_monitoring" title="Hydraulic fracturing">Hydraulic_fracturing \xc2\xa7&#160;Fracture monitoring</a></div>\n<p>The hydraulic fracturing process creates a large number of microseismic events, which require monitoring.<sup id="cite_ref-NERC2015_21-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NERC2015-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\nA 2012 research paper from <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.refine.org.uk/">ReFINE</a> concludes that the maximum recorded fracture height in US shale plays is 588 metres.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Microseismic_monitoring">Microseismic monitoring</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Microseismic monitoring">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Microseismic monitoring techniques, using very sensitive microphones and tilt meters can monitor the growth of fractures in the target formation in real time. This can be done using a surface array, or, if there is a nearby offset well, using downhole microphones.  This means that the engineers can modify the pump rate based upon the growth of the fractures, and stop pumping if there is evidence of vertical migration into faults. This technology is available from many big oilfield service companies.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Areas_of_use">Areas of use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Areas of use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Shale_gas_in_the_United_Kingdom#Areas" title="Shale gas in the United Kingdom">Shale gas in the United Kingdom \xc2\xa7&#160;Areas</a></div>\n<p>Only high volume hydraulic fracturing<sup id="cite_ref-EA_EXPLORE_REGS_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EA_EXPLORE_REGS-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> combined with <a href="/wiki/Horizontal_drilling" class="mw-redirect" title="Horizontal drilling">horizontal drilling</a> is likely to enable commercial extraction of unconventional hydrocarbon resources, such as shale gas and <a href="/wiki/Light_tight_oil" class="mw-redirect" title="Light tight oil">light tight oil</a>, in the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-RAEreport_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RAEreport-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-decc1213_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-decc1213-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-postpn374_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-postpn374-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>   \nThe areas where hydraulic fracturing are expected to be used are the <a href="/wiki/Bowland_Shale_Formation" title="Bowland Shale Formation">Upper Bowland Shale</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Pennine_Basin" title="Pennine Basin">Pennine Basin</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lancashire" title="Lancashire">Lancashire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire" title="Yorkshire">Yorkshire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-postpn374_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-postpn374-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> and the Jurassic oil-bearing shales of the <a href="/wiki/Weald_Basin#Economic_resources" title="Weald Basin">Weald Basin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The national parks with geologies of possible interest are the <a href="/wiki/North_York_Moors" title="North York Moors">North York Moors</a> (shales), the <a href="/wiki/Peak_District" title="Peak District">Peak District</a> (shales and coals), the <a href="/wiki/South_Downs" title="South Downs">South Downs</a> (shale oil) and to the south of the <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire_Dales" title="Yorkshire Dales">Yorkshire Dales</a> (shales and coals).<sup id="cite_ref-durham-np_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-durham-np-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Eden_Project" title="Eden Project">Eden Project</a> in Cornwall is in the process of drilling and hydraulically fracturing two geothermal wells for utilisation of <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_energy" title="Geothermal energy">geothermal energy</a> as a source for a <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_power_station" class="mw-redirect" title="Geothermal power station">geothermal power station</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulation">Regulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Regulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Regulation_of_hydraulic_fracturing" title="Regulation of hydraulic fracturing">Regulation of hydraulic fracturing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shale_gas_in_the_United_Kingdom#Regulation" title="Shale gas in the United Kingdom">Shale gas in the United Kingdom \xc2\xa7&#160;Regulation</a></div>\n<p>Several government agencies, departments and one government company are involved in the regulation of hydraulic fracturing in the United Kingdom: the Oil and Gas Authority (OGA),<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> the Department for Business Energy and Industrial Strategy (BEIS), the local council planning authority including the Minerals Planning Authority (MPA), the Health and Safety Executive and one of four Environment Agencies<sup id="cite_ref-OOGSG_Aug16_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OOGSG_Aug16-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>2</span></sup> These environmental agencies are: the Environment Agency for England; National Resources for Wales;<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> the Scottish Environment Protection Agency (SEPA)<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> for Scotland, and; the Northern Ireland Environment Agency (NIEA)<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> for Northern Ireland.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulation_of_hydraulic_fracturing">Regulation of hydraulic fracturing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Regulation of hydraulic fracturing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Before onshore hydraulic fracturing can begin, an operator will have obtained a landward licence, known as a Petroleum Exploration and Development Licence (PEDL), from the OGA.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A series of steps are then taken to obtain permissions from the landowner and <a href="/wiki/Local_planning_authority" title="Local planning authority">council planning authorities</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BEISGuidance2017_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BEISGuidance2017-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The operator then requests a permit from the Minerals Planning Authority (MPA), who together with the local planning authority, determine if an <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_assessment" title="Environmental impact assessment">environmental impact assessment (EIA)</a>, funded by the operator, is required.<sup id="cite_ref-OOGSG_Aug16_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OOGSG_Aug16-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Up to six permits, constituting the Environmental Permitting Regulations (EPR) 2010,<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> two permits "under the Water Resources Act 1991" and one permit "under the Control of Major Accident Hazards Regulations 2015" are obtained from the appropriate environmental agency, to ensure that onshore hydraulic fracturing operators fulfil strict environmental regulations.<sup id="cite_ref-OOGSG_Aug16_56-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OOGSG_Aug16-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>5</span></sup>\n</p><p>The role of the Health and Safety Executive (HSE) is to  focus on the design and integrity of the well,<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> using an independent expert known as the \'well examiner\'.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> The EA and HSE together will "inspect the next series of hydraulic fracturing operations in England and Wales."<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>An hydraulic fracture plan (HFP) is required for both conventional hydraulic fracture well stimulation and unconventional high volume hydraulic well stimulation. The HFP is agreed with OGA in consultation with the EA and HSE.<sup id="cite_ref-OGA_HFP2017_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OGA_HFP2017-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Hydraulic fracturing consent (HFC) is granted following an application to BEIS, to be reviewed by the Secretary of State,<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> and; comply with requirements to mitigate any seismic risks.<sup id="cite_ref-OGA_HFP2017_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OGA_HFP2017-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Permitted_chemicals">Permitted chemicals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Permitted chemicals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The UK\'s four environment agencies do not permit chemical additives for hydraulic fracturing fluids that are classed as hazardous to <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">groundwater</a>, as defined by Schedule 22 of Environmental Permitting (England and Wales) Regulations 2010 (EPR 2010),<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Schedule 5 of the Pollution Prevention and Control (Scotland) Regulations 2012,<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> and the EU Groundwater Directive (80/68/EEC).<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> The environmental regulator will assess every chemical before it is added to the hydraulic fracturing fluid.<sup id="cite_ref-BEISGuidance2017_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BEISGuidance2017-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The nature of each chemical, but not the concentration, must be made available to the public.<sup id="cite_ref-DECCreg_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DECCreg-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Joint Agencies Groundwater Directive Advisory Group (JAGDAG) maintains a list of substances that have been assessed as being hazardous substances or non-hazardous pollutants for the groundwater directive. Input of hazardous substances "on the basis of their <a href="/wiki/Toxicity" title="Toxicity">toxicity</a>, persistence and capacity to <a href="/wiki/Bioaccumulation" title="Bioaccumulation">bio-accumulate</a>" is not permitted into <a href="/wiki/Potable" class="mw-redirect" title="Potable">potable</a> or unpotable groundwater.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Substances which are not hazardous are potentially non-hazardous pollutants.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At the Balcombe site, the Environment Agency permitted one requested chemical <a href="/wiki/Oxirane" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxirane">oxirane</a>, while not permitting the use of <a href="/wiki/Antimony_trioxide" title="Antimony trioxide">antimony trioxide</a>, which "would be hazardous if it came into contact with groundwater".<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In March 2014, a group of conservation charities including the <a href="/wiki/RSPB" class="mw-redirect" title="RSPB">RSPB</a> and the <a href="/wiki/National_Trust" class="mw-redirect" title="National Trust">National Trust</a> released a report containing a 10-point plan for increased regulation, highlighting their concerns about hydraulic fracturing with respect to <a href="/wiki/Groundwater_pollution" title="Groundwater pollution">groundwater pollution</a>, public water supply, wastewater management and treatment both generally and within ecologically sensitive areas including <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom#National_Parks" title="Hydraulic fracturing in the United Kingdom">National Parks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> \nUKOOG, the representative body for the UK onshore oil and gas industry, pointed to "a number of critical inaccuracies" and stated that: "many of the recommendations are already in place in the UK or are in the process of being put in place" and welcomed future dialogue with conservation agencies.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In July 2014, the <a href="/wiki/Chartered_Institute_of_Environmental_Health" title="Chartered Institute of Environmental Health">Chartered Institute of Environmental Health</a> (CIEH) and <a href="/wiki/Scientists_for_Global_Responsibility" title="Scientists for Global Responsibility">Scientists for Global Responsibility</a> (SGR) published a report about hydraulic fracturing that was broadly negative. It referred to major shortcomings in regulatory oversight regarding local environmental and public health risks, the potential for undermining efforts to tackle climate change, and the possibility that the process might cause water shortages.<sup id="cite_ref-Harrison_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harrison-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The report received a negative review from an academic based upon the lead author being a <a href="/wiki/Green_Party_of_England_and_Wales" title="Green Party of England and Wales">Green Party</a> candidate, and hydraulic fracturing protestor, and the alleged selective nature of some<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="Be more specific (March 2017)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> of the data used.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In March 2015, the shale company funded Task Force on Shale Gas criticised "current regulation" as "complex and relatively unapproachable", and responsible for the public\'s lack of confidence. The Task Force on Shale Gas recommended that the regulatory requirement for an operator-funded independent well examiner<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> to be passed to a single, new government regulator, who would also "independently monitor fracking sites". UKOOG, the industry\'s trade and advocacy group, said: "public confidence in the industry is vital". The government responded: "Both the Health and Safety Executive and the Environment Agency have full authority and responsibility to monitor all shale sites - independent of the industry,"<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In June 2015, the UK regulations for hydraulic fracturing were criticised by the chemicals policy charity, CHEM Trust,<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> stating they were not sufficiently protective, and raising concerns about the reductions in funding for the regulators of fracking, like the <a href="/wiki/Environment_Agency" title="Environment Agency">Environment Agency</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> UKOOG, responded to the CHEM Trust analysis, criticised the timing of the report: "The timing of this report is clearly designed to influence local councillors" and stated that "The report includes a number of recommendations that are already part of industry common practice or regulation in the UK."<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> and CHEM Trust<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> responded.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legislation">Legislation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Legislation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Community_and_public_engagement">Community and public engagement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Community and public engagement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Community and public engagement is a legal requirement<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> of the EU Directive 2003/35/EC.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In June 2013, the industry body UKOOG issued their Shale Community Engagement Charter.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> The shale gas industry has agreed to two types of community benefit for communities hosting shale gas development, including: a one-off payment of \xc2\xa3100,000 per site, after hydraulic fracturing had taken place, and; a 1% share of production revenues; yearly operator commitment publications.<sup id="cite_ref-BEISGuidance2017_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BEISGuidance2017-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2014, the government announced its intent to create of a Shale Wealth Fund.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> The fund was originally intended to be controlled by "community trusts or councils". A consultation period solicited views from stakeholders, "individuals, organisations, such as charities; businesses; local authorities, and; community groups"; ran between August and October 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-SWF2016_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SWF2016-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In March 2016, Stephenson Halliday for the Planning Advisory Service noted that the UKOOG local community benefits scheme "fails all three of the tests" in Regulation 122(2) of the Community Infrastructure Levy Regulations 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> \nIn 2016, the chemical company INEOS committed to a "share 6% of revenues. 4% of this would go to homeowners and landowners in the immediate vicinity of a well, and a further 2% to the wider community." In terms of total revenue, Ineos have estimated that "a typical 10&#160;km by 10&#160;km development area would generate \xc2\xa3375m for the community over its lifespan".<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Infrastructure_Act_2015_Sections_43_and_50">Infrastructure Act 2015 Sections 43 and 50</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Infrastructure Act 2015 Sections 43 and 50">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Infrastructure_Act_2015" title="Infrastructure Act 2015">Infrastructure Act 2015</a> legislated onshore access for onshore and offshore extraction of shale/tight oil, shale gas<sup id="cite_ref-PNAS2016_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PNAS2016-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> and deep geothermal energy.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Section 50 of the act defined the hydraulic fracturing of "shale strata", also known as "high-volume hydraulic fracturing" as "more than 1000m<sub>3</sub> of fluid per stage, and; more than 10,000m<sub>3</sub> in total" and attached conditions that mean no hydraulic fracturing can take place at a depth shallower than 1000m in unprotected areas.\n</p><p>In order for the Secretary of State to give consent to hydraulic fracturing, legislation includes a range of conditions that operators must comply with, such as: "environmental impacts of development", including soil and air monitoring; 12 months of groundwater methane level monitoring prior to "associated" (high-volume) hydraulic fracturing; no associated hydraulic fracturing "within protected groundwater source areas";<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Onshore_Hydraulic_Fracturing_.28Protected_Areas.29_Regulations_2016"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Onshore_Hydraulic_Fracturing_(Protected_Areas)_Regulations_2016">Onshore Hydraulic Fracturing (Protected Areas) Regulations 2016</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Onshore Hydraulic Fracturing (Protected Areas) Regulations 2016">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>"The Onshore Hydraulic Fracturing (Protected Areas) Regulations 2016" prohibited "hydraulic fracturing in protected areas" - i.e. <a href="/wiki/National_parks_of_England_and_Wales" title="National parks of England and Wales">National Parks of England and Wales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Area_of_Outstanding_Natural_Beauty" title="Area of Outstanding Natural Beauty">Areas of Outstanding Natural Beauty</a>, the <a href="/wiki/The_Broads" title="The Broads">Norfolk and Suffolk Broads</a>, and <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" class="mw-redirect" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage sites</a> - at depths of less than 1200m.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_impact">Environmental impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Environmental impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_hydraulic_fracturing" title="Environmental impact of hydraulic fracturing">Environmental_impact_of_hydraulic_fracturing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shale_gas_in_the_United_Kingdom#Environmental_impact" title="Shale gas in the United Kingdom">Shale_gas_in_the_United_Kingdom \xc2\xa7&#160;Environmental_impact</a></div>\n<p>The environmental risks of hydraulic fracturing in conventional and unconventional wells: ground water contamination, surface water contamination, releases to\nair, water resource depletion, traffic, land take, noise, visual impact and seismicity.\n</p><p>Environmental impact assessments cover a wide range of concerns, including habitat damage, effect on wildlife, traffic, noise, lighting, and air pollution. This reference shows one example.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> These are presented in less detail in a \' Non Technical Summary\'.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to Professor Mair of the Royal Society, the causation of earthquakes with any significant impact or fractures reaching and contaminating drinking water, were very low risk" if adequate regulations are in place.<sup id="cite_ref-RAEreport_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RAEreport-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A report from <a href="/wiki/AMEC" class="mw-redirect" title="AMEC">AMEC</a><sup id="cite_ref-AMEC2013_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AMEC2013-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> in December 2013 covers many of the environmental issues that would arise were the shale gas industry to become highly developed.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/British_Geological_Survey" title="British Geological Survey">British Geological Survey</a> are involved with environmental monitoring.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In October 2014, EASAC stated that: "Overall, in Europe more than 1000 horizontal wells and several thousand hydraulic fracturing jobs have been executed in recent decades. None of these operations are known to have resulted in safety or environmental problems".<sup id="cite_ref-EASAC_Oct2014_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EASAC_Oct2014-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In October 2016, Amec Foster Wheeler Infrastructure Ltd (AFWI) compared the environmental impacts and risks of unconventional high volume hydraulic fracturing with conventional low volume hydraulic fracturing. The study found that volume of fluid injected and flowback were the only significant differences between conventional low volume and unconventional high volume hydraulic fracturing and that the impacts and risks for high volume hydraulic fracturing scaled up for land take, traffic, surface water contamination and water resource depletion.<sup id="cite_ref-AMWFI2016_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AMWFI2016-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Air">Air</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Air">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In February 2016, a study by the ReFINE consortium funded by the Natural Environment Research Council (NERC), <a href="/wiki/Royal_Dutch_Shell" title="Royal Dutch Shell">Shell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chevron_Corporation" title="Chevron Corporation">Chevron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ineos" title="Ineos">Ineos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Centrica" title="Centrica">Centrica</a>, found "substantial increases over the baseline""in local air quality pollutants", during the short-duration high-traffic phase which includes the delivery of hydraulic fracturing equipment, proppant, water, as well as the removal of flowback from the site. According to ReFiNE, these short-duration increases have the potential to breach local air quality standards.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> The industry group UKOOG criticised the ReFiNE study for failing to take into account that water for hydraulic fracturing fluid might be brought in by pipeline, instead of being transported by truck.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In October 2016, Amec Foster Wheeler Infrastructure Ltd stated that the overall environmental impacts from low volume hydraulic fracturing to local air quality and global warming are low. Local air quality is impacted by dust and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">SO2</a> and <a href="/wiki/NOx" title="NOx">NOx</a> emissions "from equipment and vehicles used to transport, pressurise and injection fracturing fluids, and process flowback", while "Emissions of <a href="/wiki/CO2" class="mw-redirect" title="CO2">CO2</a> from the equipment used to pressurise and injection fracturing fluids, and process flowback." contributes to <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AFWI2016_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AFWI2016-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>161</span></sup> \n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water">Water</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Water">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The RAE report stated, "Many claims of contaminated water wells due to shale gas extraction have been made. None has shown evidence of chemicals found in hydraulic fracturing fluids".<sup id="cite_ref-RAEreport_1-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RAEreport-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> \nThe Environment Agency definitions of groundwater and aquifer are here.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In January 2015, the <a href="/wiki/British_Geological_Survey" title="British Geological Survey">British Geological Survey</a> released national baseline methane levels, which showed a wide range of readings<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Poor surface well sealing, which allows methane to leak, methane was identified in the Royal Academy of Engineering report as a risk to groundwater.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>  This was incorporated into the Infrastructure Act 2015 with a requirement that monitoring takes place 12 months before fracturing.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Chartered_Institution_of_Water_and_Environmental_Management" title="Chartered Institution of Water and Environmental Management">Chartered Institution of Water and Environmental Management</a> (CIWEM) have been involved with evaluating the potential water impacts of hydraulic fracturing.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="Reader shouldn&#39;t have to click through to link to find out what CIWEM specifically said about hydraulic fracturing and the implications for water (April 2017)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Groundwater_contamination">Groundwater contamination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Groundwater contamination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Shale_gas_in_the_United_Kingdom#Groundwater_contamination" title="Shale gas in the United Kingdom">Shale gas in the United Kingdom \xc2\xa7&#160;Groundwater contamination</a></div>\n<p>Both low and high volume hydraulic fracturing "involve storing and injecting large quantities of chemicals". Any surface spill therefore has "the potential to penetrate groundwater". The likelihood of low volume and high volume hydraulic fracturing contaminating groundwater by surface spills of stored chemicals is rare, however the risk and consequences are moderate.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> To mitigate the risk, the Environment Agency requires chemical and fluid proof well pads.<sup id="cite_ref-OOGSG_Aug16_56-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OOGSG_Aug16-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>  \nThe 2012 joint Royal Society and Royal Academy of Engineering report indicated that the distances between potable water supplies and fractured formation in various US shale plays is large, meaning the risk of contamination is very small. No cases of pollution by this route have been identified.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Another 2013 paper from ReFine indicated the potential for surface gas leaks from abandoned wells<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="UK_and_US_water_differences">UK and US water differences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: UK and US water differences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Treated mains water is the norm in the UK, and standards are required by legislation to be high. As such any pollution would have to be removed by the water companies by law. Private water wells are rare, around 62,000 households, out of 23.4 million households or 2.6%.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> In rural areas of the US, private wells are common (15%), and small communities are served by investor-owned utilities, or community schemes. UK households would therefore be expected to be less at risk than <a href="/wiki/Water_supply_and_sanitation_in_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Water supply and sanitation in the United States">those in the US</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="The material near this tag possibly contains original research. (March 2017)">original research?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>In the US, baseline methane measurements were not made at the start of the shale gas boom, meaning that it became difficult to prove whether a gas problem was due to a leaking well, or was naturally occurring.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_use">Water use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Water use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Water use is regulated by the <a href="/wiki/Environment_Agency" title="Environment Agency">EA</a> (England), the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Environment_Protection_Agency" title="Scottish Environment Protection Agency">SEPA</a> (Scotland), the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Environment_Agency" title="Northern Ireland Environment Agency">NIEA</a> (Northern Ireland) and <a href="/wiki/Natural_Resources_Wales" title="Natural Resources Wales">NRW</a> (Wales) to ensure environmental needs are not compromised.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>  Water companies assess how much water is available, before providing it to operators.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The amount of water abstracted nationally is at around 9.4 billion cubic metres.<sup id="cite_ref-H2OAbstraction_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H2OAbstraction-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> In 2015, the EA indicated that water usage at a peak level<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="what is peak level and is there a reference for this? (March 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> would be 0.1% of national use and hydraulic fracturing may use up to "30 million litres per well".<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Drier areas, such as south-east England,<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> are concerned about the impact of hydraulic fracturing on water supplies.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Seismicity">Seismicity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Seismicity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>As of August 2016, there have been two cases in the United Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_hydraulic_fracturing#Induced_seismicity_from_hydraulic_fracturing" title="Environmental impact of hydraulic fracturing">fault reactivation by hydraulic fracturing</a> that caused induced seismicity strong enough to be felt by humans at the surface: both in Lancashire (M 2.3 and M 1.5).<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In December 2015, the <a href="/wiki/Centre_for_Research_into_Earth_Energy_Systems" title="Centre for Research into Earth Energy Systems">Centre for Research into Earth Energy Systems (CeREES)</a> at Durham University published the first research of its kind, prior to "planned shale gas and oil exploitation", in order to establish a baseline for anthropogenic, <a href="/wiki/Induced_seismicity" title="Induced seismicity">induced seismic</a> events in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Preese_Hall.2C_Lancashire"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Preese_Hall,_Lancashire">Preese Hall, Lancashire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Preese Hall, Lancashire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In May 2011, the government suspended Cuadrilla\'s<sup id="cite_ref-AMEC2013_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AMEC2013-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FT_01Jun2011_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FT_01Jun2011-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MMenzies_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MMenzies-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> hydraulic fracturing operations in their Preese Hall 1 well in Lancashire, after  two small earthquakes were triggered, one of magnitude M 2.3.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_08Jun2012_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_08Jun2012-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> The largest coseismic slip caused minor deformation of the wellbore<sup id="cite_ref-well_deform_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-well_deform-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> and was strong enough to be felt.<sup id="cite_ref-White_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-White-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The company\'s temporary halt was pending DECC guidance on the conclusions of a study<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> being carried out by the <a href="/wiki/British_Geological_Survey" title="British Geological Survey">British Geological Survey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Keele_University" title="Keele University">Keele University</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FT_01Jun2011_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FT_01Jun2011-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> which concluded in April 2012 that the process posed a seismic risk minimal enough to allow it to proceed with stricter monitoring.<sup id="cite_ref-FiHa_17Apr2012_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FiHa_17Apr2012-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Cuadrilla pointed out that a number of such small-magnitude earthquakes occur naturally each month in Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Cuadrilla commissioned an investigation into the seismic activity, which concluded that the tremors were probably caused by the lubrication of an existing <a href="/wiki/Fault_(geology)" title="Fault (geology)">fault plane</a> by the unintended spread of hydraulic fracturing fluid below ground.<sup id="cite_ref-Bowland_02Nov2011_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bowland_02Nov2011-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Science_02Nov2011_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Science_02Nov2011-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBC_02Nov2011_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_02Nov2011-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2012, a report on hydraulic fracturing produced jointly by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> and the Royal Academy of Engineering noted that earthquakes of magnitude M 3.0, which are more intense than the larger of the two quakes caused by Cuadrilla are: "Felt by few people at rest or in the upper floors of buildings; similar to the passing of a truck."<sup id="cite_ref-RAEreport_1-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RAEreport-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> The British Geological Survey has published information on seismic issues relating to hydraulic fracturing.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In February 2014, following the small seismic event in the Preese Hall 1 well, and much research, the DECC issued a statement on earthquake risk.<sup id="cite_ref-DECCFeb2014_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DECCFeb2014-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Subsidence">Subsidence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Subsidence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>There is no documented evidence of hydraulic fracturing leading to subsidence.<sup id="cite_ref-DECCFeb2014_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DECCFeb2014-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Operations are commonly monitored with tiltmeters, and no compaction issues have been documented. Given the mechanical properties of unconventional rocks (their densities, low porosities, low Biot coefficients, and high stiffness), compaction is very unlikely to occur during hydrocarbon extraction.<sup id="cite_ref-aboutShale_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aboutShale-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>18</span></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Insurance">Insurance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Insurance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In an answer to questions from the \'Lets talk about Shale\'<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> initiative, run by <a href="/wiki/Westbourne_Communications" title="Westbourne Communications">Westbourne Communications</a><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> for the industry body, UKOOG, they have stated "According to the Association of British Insurers there is, at present, little evidence of a link between shale gas and property damage, and they are not aware of any claims where seismic activity as a result of fracking has been cited as a cause of damage. Damage as a result of earthquakes, subsidence, heave and landslip are all covered, in general, under buildings insurance. Insurers will continue to monitor the situation for the potential for fracking, or similar explorations, to cause damage."<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>It was reported in early 2015 that farms would not be covered by issues that may arise due to hydraulic fracturing. A clarification by the insurer indicated that this would only apply to a farmer that permitted this on their land. Surrounding farms would be covered.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In March 2017, the <a href="/wiki/Chartered_Insurance_Institute" title="Chartered Insurance Institute">Chartered Insurance Institute</a> (CII) released a report by the CII Claims Faculty New Generation Group, which explored the <i>Insurance implications of fracking</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> The authors examined the "key perils associated with fracking such as earthquakes, explosions and fire, pollution, injury and death", and found that while "most insurances policies" provided "cover for these risks", "fracking will pose additional complications around liability". The authors also considered that if widespread fracking were to lead to increased claims, "then insurers may have to consider how they underwrite this emerging higher-risk group". The authors recommended: working together within the insurance profession "to monitor and discuss the issues" while remaining "open and transparent about the risks of fracking", and; working with the "energy industry and the government" "to reduce the likelihood of potential risks occurring". The CII emphasised that "insurers need to be prepared for claims in the event of a fracking-related loss and consider policy wordings with increased fracking in mind".<sup id="cite_ref-27_March_2017_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27_March_2017-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_health">Public health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydraulic_fracturing_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Public health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>If the Minerals Planning Authority determine that public health will be significantly impacted, the Director of Public health is consulted so that a "health impact assessment" can be prepared. The Environment Agency then uses the health impact assessment when considering the "potential health effects" during their "permit determination"<sup id="cite_ref-OOGSG_Aug16_56-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OOGSG_Aug16-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>9</span></sup>\n</p><p>In 2014, Public Health England reviewed the "available evidence on issues including air quality, radon gas, naturally occurring radioactive materials, water contamination and waste water.
